<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The Brassica oleracea genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2014 5:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2014-05-23"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2014 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Polyploidization has provided much genetic variation for plant adaptive evolution, but the mechanisms by which the molecular evolution of polyploid genomes establishes genetic architecture underlying species differentiation are unclear. Brassica is an ideal model to increase knowledge of polyploid evolution. Here we describe a draft genome sequence of Brassica oleracea, comparing it with that of its sister species B. rapa to reveal numerous chromosome rearrangements and asymmetrical gene loss in duplicated genomic blocks, asymmetrical amplification of transposable elements, differential gene co-retention for specific pathways and variation in gene expression, including alternative splicing, among a large number of paralogous and orthologous genes. Genes related to the production of anticancer phytochemicals and morphological variations illustrate consequences of genome duplication and gene divergence, imparting biochemical and morphological variation to B. oleracea. This study provides insights into Brassica genome evolution and will underpin research into the many important crops in this genus. Brassica oleracea is plant species comprising economically important vegetable crops. Here, the authors report the draft genome sequence of B. oleracea and, through a comparative analysis with the closely related B. rapa, reveal insights into Brassicaevolution and divergence of interspecific genomes and intraspecific subgenomes.
                  
                    
                  
                "/>

        <meta property="og:description" content="Brassica oleracea is plant species comprising economically important vegetable crops. Here, the authors report the draft genome sequence of B. oleracea and, through a comparative analysis with the closely related B. rapa, reveal insights into Brassicaevolution and divergence of interspecific genomes and intraspecific subgenomes."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Polyploidization has provided much genetic variation for plant adaptive evolution, but the mechanisms by which the molecular evolution of polyploid genomes establishes genetic architecture underlying species differentiation are unclear. <i>Brassica</i> is an ideal model to increase knowledge of polyploid evolution. Here we describe a draft genome sequence of <i>Brassica oleracea</i>, comparing it with that of its sister species <i>B. rapa</i> to reveal numerous chromosome rearrangements and asymmetrical gene loss in duplicated genomic blocks, asymmetrical amplification of transposable elements, differential gene co-retention for specific pathways and variation in gene expression, including alternative splicing, among a large number of paralogous and orthologous genes. Genes related to the production of anticancer phytochemicals and morphological variations illustrate consequences of genome duplication and gene divergence, imparting biochemical and morphological variation to <i>B. oleracea</i>. This study provides insights into <i>Brassica</i> genome evolution and will underpin research into the many important crops in this genus.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>B<i>rassica oleracea</i> comprises many important vegetable crops including cauliflower, broccoli, cabbages, Brussels sprouts, kohlrabi and kales. The species demonstrates extreme morphological diversity and crop forms, with various members grown for their leaves, flowers and stems. About 76 million tons of <i>Brassica</i> vegetables were produced in 2010, with a value of 14.85 billion dollars ( <a href="http://faostat.fao.org/">http://faostat.fao.org/</a>). Most <i>B. oleracea</i> crops are high in protein<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="U.S. Department of Agriculture, Agricultural Research Service. USDA National Nutrient Database for Standard Reference, Release 26-Vegetables and Vegetable Products. (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1840">1</a></sup> and carotenoids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Kopsell, D. A. &amp; Kopsell, D. E. Accumulation and bioavailability of dietary carotenoids in vegetable crops. Trends Plant Sci. 11, 499â507 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1845">2</a></sup>, and contain diverse glucosinolates (GSLs) that function as unique phytochemicals for plant defence against fungal and bacterial pathogens<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Halkier, B. A. &amp; Gershenzon, J. Biology and biochemistry of glucosinolates. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 57, 303â333 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1849">3</a></sup> and on consumption have been shown to have potent anticancer properties<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Khwaja, F. S., Wynne, S., Posey, I. &amp; Djakiew, D. 3,3'-diindolylmethane induction of p75NTR-dependent cell death via the p38 mitogen-activated protein kinase pathway in prostate cancer cells. Cancer Prev. Res. (Phila) 2, 566â571 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1853">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Li, Y. et al. Sulforaphane, a dietary component of broccoli/broccoli sprouts, inhibits breast cancer stem cells. Clin. Cancer Res. 16, 2580â2590 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1856">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Higdon, J. V., Delage, B., Williams, D. E. &amp; Dashwood, R. H. Cruciferous vegetables and human cancer risk: epidemiologic evidence and mechanistic basis. Pharmacol Res. 55, 224â236 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1859">6</a></sup>.</p><p><i>B. oleracea</i> is a member of the family <i>Brassicaceae</i> (~\n338 genera and 3,709 species)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Warwick, S. I., Francis, A. &amp; Al-Shehbaz, I. A. Brassicaceae: species checklist and database on CD-Rom. Pl. Syst. Evol. 259, 249â258 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1871">7</a></sup> and one of three diploid <i>Brassica</i> species in the classical triangle of U<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Nagaharu, U. Genome analysis in Brassica with special reference to the experimental formation of B. napus and peculiar mode of fertilication. Jap. J. Bot. 7, 389â452 (1935)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1878">8</a></sup> that also includes diploids <i>B. rapa</i> (AA) and <i>B. nigra</i> (BB) and allotetraploids <i>B. juncea</i> (AABB), <i>B. napus</i> (AACC) and <i>B. carinata</i> (BBCC). These allotetraploid species are important oilseed crops, accounting for 12% of world edible oil production ( <a href="http://faostat.fao.org/">http://faostat.fao.org/</a>). As the origin and relationship between these species is clear, the timing and nature of the evolutionary events associated with <i>Brassica</i> divergence and speciation can be revealed by interspecific genome comparison. Each of the <i>Brassica</i> genomes retains evidence of recursive whole-genome duplication (WGD) events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Bowers, J. E., Chapman, B. A., Rong, J. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Unravelling angiosperm genome evolution by phylogenetic analysis of chromosomal duplication events. Nature 422, 433â438 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1912">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97â100 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1915">10</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>) and have undergone a <i>Brassiceae</i>-lineage-specific whole-genome triplication (WGT)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1925">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Lysak, M. A., Koch, M. A., Pecinka, A. &amp; Schubert, I. Chromosome triplication found across the tribe Brassiceae. Genome Res. 15, 516â525 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1928">12</a></sup> since their divergence from the <i>Arabidopsis</i> lineage. These events were followed by diploidization that involved substantial genome reshuffling and gene losses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1936">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Lysak, M. A., Koch, M. A., Pecinka, A. &amp; Schubert, I. Chromosome triplication found across the tribe Brassiceae. Genome Res. 15, 516â525 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1939">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the Mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541â1554 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1942">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Town, C. D. et al. Comparative genomics of Brassica oleracea and Arabidopsis thaliana reveal gene loss, fragmentation, and dispersal after polyploidy. Plant Cell 18, 1348â1359 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1945">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Mun, J. H. et al. Genome-wide comparative analysis of the Brassica rapa gene space reveals genome shrinkage and differential loss of duplicated genes after whole genome triplication. Genome Biol. 10, R111 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1948">15</a></sup>. Because of this, <i>Brassica</i> species are a model for the study of polyploid genome evolution (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>), mechanisms of duplicated gene loss, neo- and sub-functionalization, and associated impact on morphological diversity and species differentiation.</p><p>We report a draft genome sequence of <i>B. oleracea</i> and its comprehensive genomic comparison with the genome of sister species <i>B. rapa</i>, which diverged from a common ancestor ~\n4 MYA. These data provide insights into the dynamics of <i>Brassica</i> genome evolution and divergence, and serve as important resources for <i>Brassica</i> vegetable and oilseed crop breeding. Furthermore, this genome will support studies of the large range of morphological variation found within <i>B. oleracea</i>, which includes sexually compatible crops such as cabbages, cauliflower and broccoli that are important for their economic, nutritional and potent anticancer value.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3"><i>B. oleracea</i> genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>Complementing the sequencing of the smaller <i>B. rapa</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d21598e1994">11</a></sup>, a draft genome assembly of <i>B. oleracea</i> var. <i>capitata</i> line 02â12 was produced by interleaving Illumina, Roche 454 and Sanger sequence data. This assembly represents 85% of the estimated 630âMb genome, and includes &gt;98% of the gene space (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Methods</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Tables 1â3, 7 and 8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). The assembly was anchored to a new genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wang, W. et al. Construction and analysis of a high-density genetic linkage map in cabbage (Brassica oleracea L. var. capitata). BMC Genomics 13, 523 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d21598e2014">16</a></sup> to produce nine pseudo-chromosomes that account for 72% of the assembly, and validated by comparison with a <i>B. oleracea</i> physical map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Wang, X. et al. A physical map of Brassica oleracea shows complexity of chromosomal changes following recursive paleopolyploidizations. BMC Genomics 12, 470 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d21598e2021">17</a></sup>, a high-density <i>B. napus</i> genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Bancroft, I. et al. Dissecting the genome of the polyploid crop oilseed rape by transcriptome sequencing. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 762â766 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d21598e2029">18</a></sup> and complete BAC sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Figs 4â9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Tables 4 and 5</a>). For comparative analyses, identical genome annotation pipelines were used for annotation of protein-coding genes and transposable elements (TEs) for <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i>.</p><p>A total of 45,758 protein-coding genes were predicted, with a mean transcript length of 1,761âbp, a mean coding length of 1,037âbp, and a mean of 4.55 exons per gene (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Tab1">Table 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Methods</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>), similar to <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Arabidopsis Genome and Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796â815 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d21598e2062">19</a></sup> and <i>B. rapa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d21598e2069">11</a></sup>. Publicly available ESTs, together with RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) data generated in this study, support 94% of predicted gene models (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Tables 7 and 8</a>), and 91.6% of predicted genes have a match in at least one public protein database (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Tables 9 and 10</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). Of the 45,758 predicted genes, 13,032 produce alternative splicing (AS) variants with intron retention and exon skipping (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 11</a>). Genome annotation also predicted 3,756 non-coding RNAs (miRNA, tRNA, rRNA and snRNA) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 12</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of genome assembly and annotation of <i>B. oleracea</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms4930/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A combination of structure-based analyses and homology-based comparisons resulted in the identification of 13,382 TEs with clearly identified terminal boundaries, including 5,107 retrotransposons and 8,275 DNA transposons (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Methods</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 13</a>). These elements together with numerous truncated elements or TE remnants make up 38.80% of the assembled portion of the <i>B. oleracea</i> genome, whereas TEs account for only 21.47% of the <i>B. rapa</i> genome assembly. Copia (11.64%) and gypsy (7.84%) retroelements are the major constituents of the repetitive fraction, and are unevenly distributed across each chromosome, with retrotransposons predominantly found in pericentromeric or heterochromatic regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>) in <i>B. oleracea</i>. Tentative physical positions of some of the centromeres were determined based on homologue and phylogenetic analysis of the centromere-specific 76âbp tandem repeats CentBo-1 and CentBo-2 and copia-type retrotransposon (CentCRBo) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 14</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Figs 14â17</a>). The distribution of 45S and 5S rDNA sequences were also visualized by fluorescent <i>in situ</i> hybridization (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Figs 18 and 19</a>), leading to a predicted karyotype ideogram for <i>B. oleracea</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 20</a>). An extra-centromeric locus with colocalized centromeric satellite repeat CentBo-1 and the centromeric retrotransposon CRBo-1 was observed on the long arm of chromosome 6 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Figs 18â20</a>). A comprehensive database for the genome information is accessible at <a href="http://www.ocri-genomics.org/bolbase/index.html">http://www.ocri-genomics.org/bolbase/index.html</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Conserved syntenic blocks and genome rearrangement after WGT</h3><p>The relatively complete triplicated regions in <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i> were constructed and they relate to the 24 ancestral crucifer blocks (AâX) in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Schranz, M. E., Lysak, M. A. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. The ABCâs of comparative genomics in the Brassicaceae: building blocks of crucifer genomes. Trends Plant Sci. 11, 535â542 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d21598e2836">20</a></sup>. Further the triplicated blocks resulting from WGT in the two <i>Brassica</i> species were partitioned into three subgenomes: LF (Least-fractionated), MF1 (Medium-fractionated) and MF2 (Most-fractionated)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d21598e2843">11</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Methods</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Tables 15 and 16</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Figs 21â26</a>). These syntenic blocks occupy the majority of the genome assemblies of <i>A. thaliana</i> (19,628 genes, 72.24% of 27,169 genes), <i>B. oleracea</i> (26,485 genes, 57.88%) and <i>B. rapa</i> (26,698 genes, 64.84%), and provide a foundation for comparative analyses of chromosomal rearrangement, gene loss and divergence of retained paralogues after WGT. Massive gene loss occurred in an asymmetrical and reciprocal fashion in the three subgenomes of each species and was largely completed before the <i>B. oleraceaâB. rapa</i> divergence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Tables 17â19</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Figs 25â27</a>). The timing of this evolutionary process was supported by the estimated timing of WGT ~\n15.9 million years ago (MYA), and species divergence ~\n4.6 MYA, based on synonymous substitution (Ks) rates of genes located in the blocks (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 20</a>). Gene loss occurred mainly through small deletions that may be caused by illegitimate recombination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Woodhouse, M. R. et al. Following tetraploidy in maize, a short deletion mechanism removed genes preferentially from one of the two homologs. PLoS Biol. 8, e1000409 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d21598e2889">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Devos, K. M., Brown, J. K. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Genome size reduction through illegitimate recombination counteracts genome expansion in Arabidopsis. Genome Res. 12, 1075â1079 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d21598e2892">22</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 27</a>), consistent with observations in other plant genomes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Genomic structure and gene retention rates in syntenic regions of B. oleracea and B. rapa."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Genomic structure and gene retention rates in syntenic regions of <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4930/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4930/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4930_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4930/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4930_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="492"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Segmental colinearity of the genomes of <i>B. oleracea</i>, <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>. Syntenic blocks are defined and labelled from A to X (coloured) previously reported in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Schranz, M. E., Lysak, M. A. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. The ABCâs of comparative genomics in the Brassicaceae: building blocks of crucifer genomes. Trends Plant Sci. 11, 535â542 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d21598e2932">20</a></sup>. (<b>b</b>) Time estimate of WGD and subsequent two <i>Brassica</i> species divergence. (<b>c</b>) Pattern of retention/loss of orthologous genes on each set of three subgenomic (LF, MF1 and MF2) blocks of <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i> corresponding to <i>A. thaliana</i> A to X blocks. The <i>x</i> axis denotes the physical position of each <i>A. thaliana</i> gene locus. The <i>y</i> axis denotes the proportion of orthologous genes retained in the <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i> subgenomic blocks around each <i>A. thaliana</i> gene, where 500 genes flanking each side of a certain gene locus were analysed, giving a total window size of 1,001 genes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4930/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Abundant genome rearrangement following WGT and subsequent <i>Brassica</i> species divergence resulted in complex mosaics of triplicated ancestral genomic blocks in the A and C genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 28</a>). At least 19 major, and numerous fine-scale, chromosome rearrangements occurred, which differentiate the two <i>Brassica</i> species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 29</a>). This is in agreement with previous comparative studies based on chromosome painting<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Lysak, M. A., Koch, M. A., Pecinka, A. &amp; Schubert, I. Chromosome triplication found across the tribe Brassiceae. Genome Res. 15, 516â525 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3001">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Lysak, M. A., Cheung, K., Kitschke, M. &amp; Bures, P. Ancestral chromosomal blocks are triplicated in Brassiceae species with varying chromosome number and genome size. Plant Physiol. 145, 402â410 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3004">23</a></sup> and genetic mapping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Panjabi, P. et al. Comparative mapping of Brassica juncea and Arabidopsis thaliana using Intron Polymorphism (IP) markers: homoeologous relationships, diversification and evolution of the A, B and C Brassica genomes. BMC Genomics 9, 113 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3008">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Parkin, I. A. et al. Segmental structure of the Brassica napus genome based on comparative analysis with Arabidopsis thaliana. Genetics 171, 765â781 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3011">25</a></sup>. The extensive chromosome reshuffling in <i>Brassica</i> is in contrast to that observed in other taxa, such as the highly syntenic tomatoâpotato and pearâapple genomes, each with longer divergence times and less genome rearrangement<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Wu, J. et al. The genome of the pear (Pyrus bretschneideri Rehd.). Genome Res. 23, 396â408 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3018">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="The Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3021">27</a></sup>. This difference may be a consequence of mesopolyploidy in <i>Brassica</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Greater TEs accumulation in <i>B. oleracea</i> than <i>B. rapa</i></h3><p>Both retro- (22.13%) and DNA (16.67%) TEs appear to be greater amplified in <i>B. oleracea</i> relative to <i>B. rapa</i> (9.43 and 12.04%) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 13</a>). We constructed 1,362 gap-free contig-contig syntenic regions by clustering orthologous <i>B. rapa</i>â<i>B. oleracea</i> genes using MCscan (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Figs 29 and 30</a>). The <i>B. oleracea</i> TE length (34.03% of the 259.6M) is 3.4 times greater than that of the syntenic <i>B. rapa</i> regions (16.73% of the 155.0M) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Tables 21 and 22</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 31</a>). Phylogenetic analysis revealed that <i>B. oleracea</i> has both more LTR retrotransposon (LTR-RT) families, and more members in most families than <i>B. rapa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig2">Fig. 2d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Figs 12, 32 and 33</a>). Furthermore, two new lineages of LTR-RTs, <i>Brassica Copia</i> Retrotransposon and <i>Brassica Gypsy</i> Retrotransposon, were defined in both <i>Brassica</i> species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 33</a>). Analysis of LTR insertion time revealed that ~\n98% of <i>B. oleracea</i> intact LTR-RTs amplified continuously over the ~\n4 million years (MY) since the <i>B. oleracea</i>â<i>B. rapa</i> split, whereas ~\n68% of <i>B. rapa</i> intact LTR-RTs amplified rapidly within the last 1 MY, predominantly in the recent 0.2 MY (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 34</a>). Hence, LTR-RTs expanded more in the intergenic space of euchromatic regions in <i>B. oleracea</i> than <i>B. rapa</i>. This agrees with previous observations based on comparison of BAC sequences between the A and C genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Cheung, F. et al. Comparative analysis between homoeologous genome segments of Brassica napus and its progenitor species reveals extensive sequence-level divergence. Plant Cell 21, 1912â1928 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3130">28</a></sup>. As a consequence of continuous TE amplification over the last 4 MY, the genome size of <i>B. oleracea</i> is ~\n30% larger than that of <i>B. rapa</i> although the two genomes share the same ploidy and are largely collinear.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="TE comparison analyses in B. oleracea and B. rapa."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: TE comparison analyses in <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa.</i></b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4930/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4930/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4930_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4930/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4930_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="516"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) TE copy number and total length in each assembly and <i>B. oleraceaâB. rapa</i> syntenic blocks. (<b>b</b>) The number of intact LTR (<i>Copia</i>-like and <i>Gypsy</i>-like) birthed at different times (million years ago, MYA) in the syntenic regions of <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i>. (<b>c</b>) The comparison of TE distribution and composition in <i>B. oleraceaâB. rapa</i> syntenic blocks along <i>B. oleracea</i> chromosomes. We divided <i>B. oleraceaâB. rapa</i> syntenic region into non-overlapping sliding 200âkb windows to compare TE contents. For each window, the ratio log<sub>10</sub>(<i>B. oleracea/B. rapa</i>) was calculated for total syntenic block length (blue line), LTR length (purple line), gene length (yellow point), exons length (red point) and intron length (green point). If <i>B. oleracea</i> &gt; <i>B. rapa</i> in absolute length of TE composition in a compared window, the dot or line is above the line <i>y</i>=0. The corresponding <i>B. rapa</i> chromosome segments along <i>B. oleracea</i> C08 were indicated by coloured bars. All other <i>B. oleracea</i> chromosomes are showed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 31</a>. (<b>d</b>) Phylogeny of the Copia-like elements as an example of LTR-RTs of the syntenic regions in <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i>. The neighbor-joining (<i>NJ</i>) trees were generated based on the conserved RT domain nucleotide sequences using the Kimura two-parameter method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Kimura, M. A simple method for estimating evolutionary rates of base substitutions through comparative studies of nucleotide sequences. J. Mol. Evol. 16, 111â120 (1980)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3234">68</a></sup> in MEGA4 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Tamura, K., Dudley, J., Nei, M. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA4: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis (MEGA) software version 4.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1596â1599 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3237">69</a>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4930/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Species-specific genes and tandemly duplicated genes</h3><p>While the genomes of <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i> are highly similar in terms of total gene clusters/sequences and the gene number in each cluster, there are also a large number of species-specific genes in the two species. A total of 66.5% (34,237 genes) of <i>B. oleracea</i> genes and 74.9% (34,324) of <i>B. rapa</i> genes were clustered into OrthoMCL groups (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 23</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 35</a>). We identified 9,832 <i>B. oleracea</i>-specific and 5,735 <i>B. rapa</i>-specific genes, of which 77% were supported by gene expression and/or a clear <i>Arabidopsis</i> homologue (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 24</a>). Of them, &gt;90% of these specific genes were validated for their absence in the counterpart genomes by reciprocal mapping of raw clean reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Tables 25 and 26</a>). Most <i>Brassica</i>-specific genes are randomly distributed along the chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Figs 36 and 37</a>). More than 80% of the species-specific genes were surrounded by non-specific genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 38</a>), suggesting that deletion of individual genes may be the major mechanism underlying gene loss and the difference in gene numbers between <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i>.</p><p>Tandem duplication produces clusters of duplicated genes and contributes to the expansion of gene families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Freeling, M. Bias in plant gene content following different sorts of duplication: tandem, whole-genome, segmental, or by transposition. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 60, 433â453 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3310">29</a></sup>. We identified 1,825, 2,111 and 1,554 gene clusters containing 4,365, 5,181 and 4,170 tandemly duplicated genes in <i>B. oleracea</i>, <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Tables 27 and 28</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 39</a>). The wide range of sequence divergence of tandem gene pairs in each species suggests that tandem gene duplication occurred continuously throughout the evolutionary history of these species, rather than in discrete bursts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Figs 40 and 41</a>). Their continuous and asymmetrical occurrence after species divergence resulted in 522, 697 and 815 species-specific tandem clusters in the three genomes. The frequency of tandem duplication is independent of the total gene content, suggesting that genome triplication has not inhibited its occurrence. Tandemly duplicated genes are preferentially enriched for gene ontology (GO) categories related to defence response and pathways related to secondary metabolism such as indole alkaloid biosynthesis and tropane, piperidine and pyridine alkaloid biosynthesis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Tables 29â32</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 42</a>). Over 44.0 and 51.9% of the NBS-encoding resistance genes are tandemly duplicated in <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 33</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="The duplicated genes derived from tandem duplication and whole-genome duplications in Brassica genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: The duplicated genes derived from tandem duplication and whole-genome duplications in <i>Brassica</i> genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4930/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4930/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4930_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4930/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4930_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="477"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) A Venn diagram showing shared and specific tandem duplication events in <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i>. (<b>b</b>,<b>c</b>) Distribution of tandem genes and WGT/WGD-derived paralogues in the KEGG pathway maps in <i>B. oleracea</i> (bol), <i>B. rapa</i> (bra) and <i>A. thaliana</i> (ath). For each KEGG pathway map, the proportion of the number of duplicated genes or paralogues to the total genes was calculated (<i>x</i> axis) and the number of maps whose tandem gene proportion fell in a range was shown on the <i>y</i> axis. (<b>d</b>) Oxidative phosphorylation pathway enriched by WGT-derived paralogous genes in the <i>Brassica</i> genomes. The gene copy number for each KO enzyme in <i>B. oleracea</i>, <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> were shown (dash-connected) under the KO enzyme number.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4930/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Biased loss and retention of genes after WGT/WGD</h3><p>Following polyploidization, reversion of gene numbers towards diploid levels through gene loss has been widely observed in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Sankoff, D., Zheng, C. &amp; Zhu, Q. The collapse of gene complement following whole genome duplication. BMC Genomics 11, 313 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3438">30</a></sup>. However, in <i>Brassica</i> this only appears to be true for collinear genes in the conserved syntenic regions, with a loss of ~\n60% of the predicted post-triplication gene set, nearly restoring the pre-triplication gene number. This is reflected in an overall retention rate of 1.2-fold of <i>A. thaliana</i> orthologous genes in corresponding syntenic regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 18</a>). In contrast, in terms of genes that have no collinear gene in <i>A. thaliana</i> and either <i>Brassica</i> species (hereafter called non-collinear genes), gene retention rates is 2.5-fold the <i>A. thaliana</i> gene number in <i>B. oleracea</i> and 1.9-fold in <i>B. rapa</i>, both significantly higher than the expected rates (<i>P</i> value &lt;2.2eâ16;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 34</a>). For these retained genes, the numbers of the genes that are common in the two <i>Brassica</i> species are 11,746 in <i>B. oleracea</i> and 10,411 in <i>B. rapa</i>. Most of these genes are supported by expression and/or the presence of an <i>Arabidopsis</i> homologue (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 35</a>). More than 61% of these genes have homologues present as collinear genes and 16% also are homologous to other non-collinear genes, indicating gene movement from triplicated syntenic regions and being similar to observations in <i>A. thaliana</i>, where half of the genes are nonsyntenic within rosids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Woodhouse, M. R., Tang, H. &amp; Freeling, M. Different gene families in Arabidopsis thaliana transposed in different epochs and at different frequencies throughout the rosids. Plant Cell 23, 4241â4253 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3496">31</a></sup>. This suggests that the breakdown of the triplicated syntenic relationship has not only prevented gene loss and a move towards pre-triplication gene numbers but has also maintained a higher gene density, and thus maintained WGT-derived genes for species evolution.</p><p>The presence of a large number of the retained paralogous genes in the syntenic regions led us to examine whether genes in some functional categories have preferentially been over-retained, as observed in other plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Freeling, M. Bias in plant gene content following different sorts of duplication: tandem, whole-genome, segmental, or by transposition. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 60, 433â453 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3503">29</a></sup>. The results indicate that WGT-produced paralogous genes are over-retained in GO categories associated with regulation of metabolic and biosynthetic processes, RNA metabolism and transcription factors (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 36</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Figs 43â45</a>), and the two <i>Brassica</i> species exhibit similar patterns of gene category retention. From a study of KEGG pathways, we also found that WGT-produced <i>Brassica</i> paralogous genes contribute 40â60% of total genes for 90% of KEGG pathways (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 43</a>), and are functionally enriched in primary or core metabolic processes such as oxidative phosphorylation, carbon fixation, photosynthesis, circadian rhythm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Lou, P. et al. Preferential retention of circadian clock genes during diploidization following whole genome triplication in Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 24, 2415â2426 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3526">32</a></sup> and lipid metabolism (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Tables 36 and 37</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Figs 43â45</a>). Notably, the pathways associated with energy metabolism have been enhanced in both <i>Brassica</i> species. For instance, in the oxidative phosphorylation pathway, there are 161 genes in <i>A. thaliana</i>, but 241 in <i>B. oleracea</i> and 208 in <i>B. rapa</i>. The majority (143/241 and 142/208) of these <i>Brassica</i> genes are multiple paralogues residing in the triplicated syntenic regions, and more than half of these paralogues have been retained as three copies, significantly higher than observed for other genes in the triplication regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 43</a>).</p><p>Phylogenetic analyses show that WGT led to an expansion of genes involved in auxin functioning (AUX, IAA, GH3, PIN, SAUR, TAA, TIR, TPL and YUCCA), morphology specification (TCP), and flowering time control (FLC, CO, VRN1, LFY, AP1 and GI) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 38</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Figs 46â61</a>), and that most <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes in these families have two or three orthologs in <i>Brassica</i> species. These WGT-produced duplicated genes may provide important sources of evolutionary innovation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Doyle, J. J. et al. Evolutionary genetics of genome merger and doubling in plants. Annu. Rev. Genet. 42, 443â461 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3573">33</a></sup> and contribute to the extreme morphological diversity in <i>Brassica</i> species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Divergence of duplicated genes in the <i>Brassica</i> genomes</h3><p>The largest genetic foundation for plant genome evolution and new species formation is the differentiation of retained paralogous and orthologous genes. Around 38% (4,302/11,493) of all paralogous gene pairs in <i>B. oleracea</i> and ~\n36% (4,089/11,448) in <i>B. rapa</i> have different predicted exon numbers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Data 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Tables 39 and 40</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 62</a>). There are 6,571 orthologous gene pairs with different exon numbers, accounting for 27.6% of total gene pairs (23,823). Some paralogous or orthologous pairs have high Ks values and low sequence similarity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 63</a>), indicating sequence differentiation. Of these paralogous genes, some offer appreciable opportunity for non-reciprocal DNA exchanges (gene conversion). About 8% of the 4,296 homologous quartets in <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i> have been affected by gene conversion (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 41</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 64</a>) and about one-sixth (53) of converted genes were inferred to have experienced independent conversion events in both <i>Brassica</i> species, a parallelism sometimes observed in other plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3631">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Wang, X., Tang, H. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Seventy million years of concerted evolution of a homoeologous chromosome pair, in parallel, in major Poaceae lineages. Plant Cell 23, 27â37 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3634">34</a></sup>. Around 40â44% of conversion events involved paralogues in the less-fractionated subgenomes LF in both species, substantially higher than the other two subgenomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 41</a>). This finding suggests that gene conversion is related to homologous gene density, which determines the likelihood of illegitimate recombination.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Divergence of Brassica paralogous and orthologous genes in B. oleracea and B. rapa."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Divergence of <i>Brassica</i> paralogous and orthologous genes in <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4930/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4930/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4930_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4930/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4930_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="718"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Genome-wide gene conversion in <i>B. oleracea</i>. The conversion in <i>B. rapa</i> is showed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 64</a>. (<b>b</b>) The ratio of differentially expressed duplicated gene pairs derived from different duplications: alpha whole-genome duplication (Î±-WGD), <i>Brassiceae</i>-lineage WGT, tandem duplication (TD). Bol, <i>B. oleracea</i>; Bra, <i>B. rapa</i>. C: callus; R: root; St: stem; L: leaf; F: flower; Si: silique. The differentially expressed duplicated gene pairs were defined as fold change &gt;2 and false discovery rate (FDR) &lt;0.05 or gene pair where expression was detected for only one gene within gene pairs (FDR &lt;0.05). (<b>c</b>) Box and whisker plots for differentiated expression for three subgenomes (LF, MF1 and MF2) in flower tissue of <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i>. For the other tissues, see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 67</a>. (<b>d</b>) The duplicated gene pairs belonging to transcription factors (TFs) and its related GO terms contain a significantly lower ratio of differentially expressed duplicated gene pairs than the average at the genome-wide level in leaf (values given) and other tissues (values not presented) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 45</a>). (<b>e</b>) The GO terms (left) in which the duplicated gene pairs contain a significantly higher ratio of differentially expressed duplicated gene pairs than the average ratio at the genome-wide level in leaf and other tissues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 46</a>). Values from one tissue were presented and the other tissues were indicated with abbreviated letters to the right if expression in these tissues is significantly higher. (<b>f</b>) Expression variation caused by divergence (either different variants or differential expression of the same variants) of alternative splicing (AS) variants in WGT paralogous gene pairs with identical numbers of exons and in BolâBra orthologous gene pairs. IRES denotes types of intron retention and exon skipping.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4930/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Analysis of RNA-seq data generated from callus, root, leaf, stem, flower and silique of <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i> suggests that &gt;40% of WGT paralogous gene pairs are differentially expressed in these species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 65</a>), suggesting potential subfunctionalization of these genes. In both species, a general trend of expression differentiation was alpha-WGD paralogous genes (~\n46%) &gt; WGT paralogous genes (~\n42%) &gt; tandemly duplicated genes (~\n35%) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 66</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Tables 42 and 43</a>). Different tissues harbour approximately the same number of differentially expressed duplicates, but this number was slightly higher in flower tissue. The expression level of genes in the LF subgenome was significantly higher than corresponding syntenic genes in the more fractionated subgenomes (MF1 and MF2) while no expression dominance relationship was observed between the subgenomes MF1 and MF2 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 44</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 67</a>). Duplicated transcription factor gene pairs showed less differentiated expression (~\n38%) than the expected ratio at the genome-wide level (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig4">Fig. 4d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 45</a>), while paralogues with GO categories related to membrane, catalytic activity and defence response exhibited a higher ratio of differentiated expression (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig4">Fig. 4e</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 46</a>). Of <i>B. oleraceaâB. rapa</i> orthologous gene pairs (23,823 in total), ~\n42% were differentially expressed across all tissues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Tables 42 and 43</a>).</p><p>Furthermore, many paralogues generate different transcripts, resulting in expression differentiation. Analysis of AS variants of paralogous gene pairs that have identical numbers of exons demonstrated that these variants (either different variants or differential expression of the same variants) cause &gt;20% and &gt;44% of such paralogous genes to be differentially expressed in <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig4">Fig. 4f</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 47</a>). For orthologous gene pairs of <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i>, 35.5% (8,467) of gene pairs showed differential expression due to AS variation. When only counting intron retention and exon skipping, 9.3% (2,215) of gene pairs differ. Divergence in AS variants of gene pairs presents an important layer of gene regulation, as reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Syed, N. H., Kalyna, M., Marquez, Y., Barta, A. &amp; Brown, J. W. Alternative splicing in plants--coming of age. Trends Plant Sci. 17, 616â623 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3799">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Gabut, M. et al. An alternative splicing switch regulates embryonic stem cell pluripotency and reprogramming. Cell 147, 132â146 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3802">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Zhang, P. G., Huang, S. Z., Pin, A. L. &amp; Adams, K. L. Extensive divergence in alternative splicing patterns after gene and genome duplication during the evolutionary history of Arabidopsis. Mol. Biol. Evol. 27, 1686â1697 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3805">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Filichkin, S. A. et al. Genome-wide mapping of alternative splicing in Arabidopsis thaliana. Genome Res. 20, 45â58 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3808">38</a></sup>, and thus provides a genetic basis for species evolution and new species formation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Unique GSLs metabolism pathways</h3><p>GSLs and hydrolysis products have been of long-standing interest due to their role in plant defence and anticancer properties. Compared with <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. napus</i>, <i>B. oleracea</i> has the greatest GSL profile diversity, with wide qualitative and quantitative variation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Yang, B. &amp; Quiros, C. F. Survey of glucosinolate variation in leaves of Brassica rapa crops. Genet. Res. Crop Evol. 57, 1079â1089 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3829">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Benderoth, M., Pfalz, M. &amp; Kroymann, J. Methylthioalkylmalate synthases: genetics, ecology and evolution. Phytochem. Rev. 8, 255â268 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3832">40</a></sup>. We identified 101 and 105 GSL biosynthesis genes in <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i>, respectively, and 22 GSL catabolism genes in each species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 48</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Data 2</a>). In the GSL biosynthesis and catabolism pathways, tandem genes (41.4%, 40.7% and 33.9% in <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i>, respectively) were present in a much higher proportion than the genome-wide average (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 32</a>). The observed variation of GSL profiles is mainly attributed to the duplication of two genes, <i>methylthioalkylmalate</i> (<i>MAM</i>) <i>synthase</i> and <i>2-oxoglutarate-dependent dioxygenase</i> (<i>AOP</i>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Whole-genome-wide comparison of genes involved in glucosinolate metabolism pathways in B. oleracea and its relatives."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Whole-genome-wide comparison of genes involved in glucosinolate metabolism pathways in <i>B. oleracea</i> and its relatives.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4930/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4930/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4930_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4930/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4930_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="561"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Aliphatic and indolic GSL biosynthesis and catabolism pathways in <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i>. The copy number of GSL biosynthetic genes in <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i> are listed in square brackets, respectively. Potential anticancer substances/precursors are highlighted in blue bold. Two important amino acid chain elongation and side-chain modification loci <i>MAMs</i> and <i>AOP2</i> are highlighted in red bold, within the number in the green bracket representing the number of non-functional genes. (<b>b</b>,<b>c</b>) The neighbour-joining (NJ) trees of MAM and AOP genes were generated based on the aligned coding sequences and 100 bootstrap repeats. The silenced genes are indicated by red hollow circle, expressed functional genes are represented by red solid disc and green rectangle. In <i>A. thaliana</i> ecotype Columbia there are just MAM1 and MAM3. (<b>d</b>) Three <i>B. oleracea AOP2</i> loci among which are one functional <i>AOP2</i> and two mutated <i>AOP2</i>. 1MOI3M: 1-methoxyindol-3-ylmethyl GSL; 1OHI3M: 1-hydroxyindol-3-ylmethyl GSL; 3MSOP: 3-methylsulfinylpropyl GSL; 3MTP: 3-methylthiopropyl GSL; 3PREY: 2-Propenyl GSL; 4BTEY: 3-butenyl GSL; 4MOI3M: 4-methoxyindol-3-ylmethyl GSL; 4OHB, 4-hydroxybutyl GSL; 4OHI3M: 4-hydroxyindol-3-ylmethyl GSL; 4MSOB: 4-methylsulfinylbutyl GSL; 4MTB, 4-methylthiobutyl GSL; AITC: allyl isothiocyanate; I3C: indole-3-carbinol; I3M: indolyl-3-methyl GSL; DIM: 3,3â²-diindolymethane; MAM: methylthioalkylmalate; AOP: 2-oxoglutarate-dependent dioxygenase.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4930/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In <i>Arabidopsis</i>, the <i>MAM</i> family contains three tandemly duplicated and functionally diverse members (<i>MAM1</i>, <i>MAM2</i> and <i>MAM3</i>), and functional analysis demonstrated that MAM2 (absent in ecotype Columbia) and MAM1 catalyses the condensation reaction of the first and the first two elongation cycles for the synthesis of dominant 3 and 4 carbon (C) side-chain aliphatic GSLs, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Benderoth, M., Pfalz, M. &amp; Kroymann, J. Methylthioalkylmalate synthases: genetics, ecology and evolution. Phytochem. Rev. 8, 255â268 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3974">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Benderoth, M. et al. Positive selection driving diversification in plant secondary metabolism. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci.
