USA 103, 9118â9123 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3977">41</a></sup>, while MAM3 is assumed to contribute to the production of all GSL chain lengths<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Textor, S., de Kraker, J. W., Hause, B., Gershenzon, J. &amp; Tokuhisa, J. G. MAM3 catalyses the formation of all aliphatic glucosinolate chain lengths in Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 144, 60â71 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d21598e3981">42</a></sup>. In <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i>, <i>MAM1</i>/<i>MAM2</i> genes experienced independent tandem duplication to produce 6 and 5 orthologs respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig5">Fig. 5b,c</a>). The main GSLs in <i>B. oleracea</i> are 4C and 3C GSLs (progoitrin, gluconapin, glucoraphanin and sinigrin)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Volden, J. et al. Processing (blanching, boiling, steaming) effects on the content of glucosinolates and antioxidant related parameters in cauliflower (Brassica oleracea L. ssp. botrytis). LWT Food Sci. Technol. 42, 63â73 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4004">43</a></sup>, while those in <i>B. rapa</i> are 4C and 5C GSLs (gluconapin and glucobrassicanapin)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Yang, B. &amp; Quiros, C. F. Survey of glucosinolate variation in leaves of Brassica rapa crops. Genet. Res. Crop Evol. 57, 1079â1089 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4011">39</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>). Based on the results of expression and phylogenetic analyses, we found a pair of genes Bol017070 and Bra013007, which are the only orthologous genes showing high expression in <i>B. oleracea</i> but silenced in <i>B. rapa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>). This expression difference most likely leads to greater accumulation of the 3C GSL anticancer precursor sinigrin in <i>B. oleracea</i>. Meanwhile, the expression level of MAM3 in <i>B. rapa</i> is much higher than in <i>B. oleracea</i>, explaining the accumulation of 5C GSL glucobrassicanapin in <i>B. rapa</i>. Other genes affecting specific anticancer GLS products are <i>AOPs.</i> Previously, research has reported four gene loci involved in the side-chain modifications of aliphatic GSLs in <i>Arabidopsis</i>. Two tandemly duplicated genes <i>AOP2</i> and <i>AOP3</i> catalyse the formation of alkenyl and hydroxyalkyl GSLs, respectively. When both <i>AOPs</i> are non-functional, the plant accumulates the precursor methylsulfinyl alkyl GSL. We identified three <i>AOP2</i> genes in <i>B. oleracea</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#Fig5">Fig. 5d</a>), but two are non-functional due to the presence of premature stop codons. In contrast, all three <i>AOP2</i> copies are functional in <i>B. rapa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Wang, H. et al. Glucosinolate biosynthetic genes in Brassica rapa. Gene 487, 135â142 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4071">44</a></sup>. No <i>AOP3</i> homologue has been identified in <i>Brassica</i>. This analysis supports GSL content surveys and explains why glucoraphanin is abundant in <i>B. oleracea</i>, but not in <i>B. rapa</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>The <i>Brassica</i> genomes experienced WGT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4100">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Lysak, M. A., Koch, M. A., Pecinka, A. &amp; Schubert, I. Chromosome triplication found across the tribe Brassiceae. Genome Res. 15, 516â525 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4103">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Parkin, I. A. et al. Segmental structure of the Brassica napus genome based on comparative analysis with Arabidopsis thaliana. Genetics 171, 765â781 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4106">25</a></sup> followed by massive gene loss and frequent reshuffling of triplicated genomic blocks. Analysis of retained or lost genes following triplication identified over-retention of genes for metabolic pathways such as oxidative phosphorylation, carbon fixation, photosynthesis and circadian rhythm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Lou, P. et al. Preferential retention of circadian clock genes during diploidization following whole genome triplication in Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 24, 2415â2426 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4110">32</a></sup>, which may contribute to polyploid vigour<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Chen, Z. J. Molecular mechanisms of polyploidy and hybrid vigour. Trends Plant Sci. 15, 57â71 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4114">45</a></sup>. Fewer lost genes were observed in the less-fractionated subgenome, possibly due to expression dominance as reported in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069â4074 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4118">46</a></sup>.</p><p>Gene expression analysis revealed extensive divergence and AS variants between duplicate genes. This subfunctionalization or neofunctionalization of duplicated genes provides genetic novelty and a basis for species evolution and new species formation. For example, TF genes that are considered to be conserved still have more than 38% of paralogous pairs showing differential expression across tissues although this percentage is lower than the average from all duplicated genes. Gene expression variation may contribute to an increased complexity of regulatory networks after polyploidization.</p><p>The multi-layered asymmetrical evolution of the <i>Brassica</i> genomes revealed in this study suggests mechanisms of polyploid genome evolution underlying speciation. Asymmetrical gene loss between the <i>Brassica</i> subgenomes, the asymmetrical amplification of TEs and tandem duplications, preferential enrichment of genes for certain pathways or functional categories, and divergence in DNA sequence and expression, including alternative splicing among a large number of paralogous and orthologous genes, together shape a route for genome evolution after polyploidization. A molecular model of polyploid genome evolution through these asymmetrical mechanisms is summarized in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>. The additional information of accessible large datasets and resource was provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Table 49</a>.</p><p>In summary, the <i>B. oleracea</i> genomic sequence, its features in comparison with its relatives, and the genome evolution mechanisms revealed, provide a fundamental resource for the genetic improvement of important traits, including components of GSLs for anticancer pharmaceuticals. The genome sequence has also laid a foundation for investigation of the tremendous range of morphological variation in <i>B. oleracea</i> as well as supporting genome analysis of the important allotetraploid crop <i>B. napus</i> (canola or rapeseed).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Sample preparation and genome sequencing</h3><p>A <i>B. oleracea</i> sp. <i>capitata</i> homozygous line 02â12 with elite agronomic characters and widely used as a parent in hybrid breeding was used for the reference genome sequencing (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Methods</a>). The seedlings of plants were collected and genomic DNA was extracted from leaves with a standard CTAB extraction method. Illumina Genome Analyser whole-genome shotgun sequencing combined with GS FLX Titanium sequencing technology was used to achieve a <i>B. oleracea</i> draft genome. We constructed a total of 35 paired-end sequencing libraries with insertion sizes of 180 base pairs (bp), 200âbp, 350âbp, 500âbp, 650âbp, 800âbp, 2âkb, 5âkb, 10âkb and 20âkb following a standard protocol provided by Illumina (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Methods</a>). Sequencing was performed using Illumina Genome Analyser II according to the manufacturerâs standard protocol.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome assembly and validation</h3><p>We took a series of checking and filtering measures on reads following the Illumina-Pipeline, and low-quality reads, adaptor sequences and duplicates were removed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Methods</a>). The reads after the above filtering and correction steps were used to perform assembly including contig construction, scaffold construction and gap filling using SOAPdenovo1.04 ( <a href="http://soap.genomics.org.cn/">http://soap.genomics.org.cn/</a>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Methods</a>). Finally, we used 20-kb-span paired-end data generated from the 454 platform and 105-kb-span BAC-end data downloaded from NCBI ( <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nucgss?term=BOT01">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nucgss?term=BOT01</a>) to extend scaffold length (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Methods</a>). The <i>B. oleracea</i> genome size was estimated using the distribution curve of 17-mer frequency (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Methods</a>).</p><p>To anchor the assembled scaffolds onto pseudo-chromosomes, we developed a genetic map using a double haploid population with 165 lines derived from a F1 cross between two homozygous lines 02â12 (sequenced) and 0188 (re-sequenced). The genetic map contains 1,227 simple sequence repeat markers and single nucleotide polymorphism markers in nine linkage groups, which span a total of 1,180.2âcM with an average of 0.96âcM between the adjacent loci<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wang, W. et al. Construction and analysis of a high-density genetic linkage map in cabbage (Brassica oleracea L. var. capitata). BMC Genomics 13, 523 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4220">16</a></sup>. To position these markers to the scaffolds, marker primers were compared with the scaffold sequences using e-PCR (parameters -n2 -g1 âd 400â800), with the best-scoring match chosen in case of multiple matches.</p><p>We validated the <i>B. oleracea</i> genome assembly by comparing it with the published physical map constructed using 73,728 BAC clones ( <a href="http://lulu.pgml.uga.edu/fpc/WebAGCoL/%20brassica/WebFPC/">http://lulu.pgml.uga.edu/fpc/WebAGCoL/brassica/WebFPC/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Wang, X. et al. A physical map of Brassica oleracea shows complexity of chromosomal changes following recursive paleopolyploidizations. BMC Genomics 12, 470 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4237">17</a></sup> and a genetic map from <i>B. napus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Bancroft, I. et al. Dissecting the genome of the polyploid crop oilseed rape by transcriptome sequencing. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 762â766 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4243">18</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Methods</a>). Eleven Sanger-sequenced <i>B. oleracea</i> BAC sequences were used to assess the assembled genome using MUMmer-3.22 ( <a href="http://mummer.sourceforge.net/">http://mummer.sourceforge.net/</a>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Methods</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>Gene prediction was performed on the genome sequence after pre-masking for TEs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Methods</a>). Gene prediction was processed with the following steps: (i) <i>De novo</i> gene prediction used AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Stanke, M., Steinkamp, R., Waack, S. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, W309âW312 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4278">47</a></sup> and GlimmerHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878â2879 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4282">48</a></sup> with parameters trained from <i>A. thaliana</i> genes. (ii) For homologue prediction, we mapped the protein sequences from <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>C. papaya</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> to the <i>B. oleracea</i> genome using tblastn with an <i>E</i>-value cutoff of 10<sup>â5</sup>, and used GeneWise (Version 2.2.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988â995 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4314">49</a></sup> for gene annotation. (iii) For EST-aided annotation, the <i>Brassica</i> ESTs from NCBI were aligned to the <i>B. oleracea</i> genome using BLAT (identity <span class="stix">â©¾</span>0.95, coverage <span class="stix">â©¾</span>0.90) and further assembled using PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Xu, Y., Wang, X., Yang, J., Vaynberg, J. &amp; Qin, J. PASAâa program for automated protein NMR backbone signal assignment by pattern-filtering approach. J. Biomol. NMR 34, 41â56 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4324">50</a></sup>. Finally, all the predictions were combined using GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Elsik, C. G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4329">51</a></sup> to produce the consensus gene sets.</p><p>Functional annotation of <i>B. oleracea</i> genes was based on comparison with SwissProt, TrEMBL, Interproscan and KEGG proteins databases. The tRNA genes were identified by tRNAscan-SE using default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955â964 (1997)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4339">52</a></sup>. Then rRNAs were compared with the genome using blastn. Other non-coding RNAs, including miRNA, snRNA, were identified using INFERNAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Nawrocki, E. P., Kolbe, D. L. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335â1337 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4343">53</a></sup> by comparison with the Rfam database.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">TE annotation</h3><p>LTR-RTs were initially identified using the LTR_STRUC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="McCarthy, E. M. &amp; McDonald, J. F. LTR_STRUC: a novel search and identification program for LTR retrotransposons. Bioinformatics 19, 362â367 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4355">54</a></sup> programme, and then manually annotated and checked based on structure characteristics and sequence homology. Refined intact elements were then used to identify other intact elements and solo LTRs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Ma, J., Devos, K. M. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Analyses of LTR-retrotransposon structures reveal recent and rapid genomic DNA loss in rice. Genome Res. 14, 860â869 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4359">55</a></sup>. All the LTR-RTs with clear boundaries and insertion sites were classified into superfamilies (<i>Copia</i>-like, <i>Gypsy</i>-like and Unclassified retroelements) and families relying on the internal protein sequence, 5â², 3â² LTRs, primer-binding site and polypurine tracts. Non-LTR-RTs (Long interspersed nuclear element, LINE and Short interspersed nuclear element, SINE) and DNA transposons (<i>Tc1-Mariner</i>, <i>hAT</i>, <i>Mutator</i>, <i>Pong</i>, <i>PIF-Harbinger</i>, CACTA and miniature inverted repeat TE) were identified using conserved protein domains of reverse transposase or transposase as queries to search against the assembled genome using tblastn. Further upstream and downstream sequences of the candidate matches were compared with each other to define their boundaries and structure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Holligan, D., Zhang, X., Jiang, N., Pritham, E. J. &amp; Wessler, S. R. The transposable element landscape of the model legume Lotus japonicus. Genetics 174, 2215â2228 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4385">56</a></sup>. <i>Helitron</i> elements were identified by the HelSearch 1.0 programme<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Yang, L. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Structure-based discovery and description of plant and animal Helitrons. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 12832â12837 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4393">57</a></sup> and manually inspected. All the TE categories were identified according to the criteria described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Wicker, T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 973â982 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4397">58</a></sup>. Typical elements of each category were selected and mixed together as a database for RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Smit, A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker. http://www.repeatmasker.org." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4401">59</a></sup> analysis. Around 20 Ã coverage of shotgun reads randomly sampled from the two <i>Brassica</i> genomes were masked by the same TE data set to confirm the different accumulation of TEs between the two genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Syntenic block construction of <i>B. oleracea</i> and its relatives</h3><p>We used the same strategy as described in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome paper<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4424">11</a></sup> to construct syntenic blocks between species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Methods</a>). The all-against-all blastp comparison (<i>E</i>-value â¤ 1eâ5) provided the gene pairs for syntenic clustering determined by MCScan (MATCH_SCORE: 50, MATCH_SIZE: 5, GAP_SCORE: â3, E_VALUE: 1Eâ05). As applied in <i>B. rapa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4436">11</a></sup>, we assigned and partitioned multiple <i>B. oleracea</i> or <i>B. rapa</i> chromosomal segments that matched the same <i>A. thaliana</i> segment (âA to Xâ numbering system in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Devos, K. M., Brown, J. K. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Genome size reduction through illegitimate recombination counteracts genome expansion in Arabidopsis. Genome Res. 12, 1075â1079 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4452">22</a></sup>) into three subgenomes: LF, MF1 and MF2.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">OrthoMCL clustering</h3><p>To identify and estimate the number of potential orthologous gene families between <i>B. oleracea</i>, <i>B. rapa</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>C. papaya</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>, and also between <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i>, we applied the OrthoMCL pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4495">60</a></sup> using standard settings (blastp <i>E</i> value &lt;1 Ã 10<sup>â5</sup> and inflation factor =1.5) to compute the all-against-all similarities.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Phylogenetic analysis of gene families</h3><p>We performed comparative analysis of trait-related gene families. Genes from grape, papaya and <i>Arabidopsis</i> were downloaded from the GenoScope database ( <a href="http://www.genoscope.cns.fr/externe/GenomeBrowser/Vitis/">http://www.genoscope.cns.fr/externe/GenomeBrowser/Vitis/</a>), the Hawaii Papaya Genome Project ( <a href="http://asgpb.mhpcc.hawaii.edu/papaya/">http://asgpb.mhpcc.hawaii.edu/papaya/</a>), and the Arabidopsis Information Resource ( <a href="http://www.arabidopsis.org/">http://www.arabidopsis.org/</a>). Previously reported <i>Arabidopsis</i> and <i>Brassica</i> gene sequences were downloaded from TAIR ( <a href="http://www.arabidopsis.org/">http://www.arabidopsis.org/</a>) and BRAD ( <a href="http://brassicadb.org/brad/">http://brassicadb.org/brad/</a>). The protein sequences of the genes were used to determine homologues in grape, papaya, <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i> by performing blast comparisons with an <i>E</i>-value 1eâ10. The Clustal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Larkin, M. A. et al. Clustal W and Clustal X version 2.0. Bioinformatics 23, 2947â2948 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4571">61</a></sup> programs were used for multiple sequence alignment. Alignment of the small family of GI genes was performed using MEGA5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Tamura, K. et al. MEGA5: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance, and maximum parsimony methods. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2731â2739 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4575">62</a></sup> to conduct neighbour-joining analysis with default parameters and subjected to careful manual checks to remove highly divergent sequences from further analysis. While for other genes, often found in families of tens of genes, the phylogenetic analysis were performed by PhyML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Guindon, S., Delsuc, F., Dufayard, J. F. &amp; Gascuel, O. Estimating maximum likelihood phylogenies with PhyML. Methods Mol. Biol. 537, 113â137 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4579">63</a></sup>, which can accommodate quite divergent sequences by implementing a maximal likelihood approach with initial analysis based on neighbour-joining method. During these analyses, we constructed trees using both CDS and protein sequence, and the protein-derived tree was used to show the phylogeny if not much incongruity was found. Bootstrapping was performed using 100 repetitive samplings for each gene family. All the inferred trees were displayed using MEGA5 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Tamura, K. et al. MEGA5: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance, and maximum parsimony methods. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2731â2739 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4582">62</a>). The multiple sequence alignment of these families was provided as <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Data 3</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Differential expression of duplicated genes across tissues</h3><p>RNA-seq reads were mapped to their respective locations on the reference genome using Tophat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562â578 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4597">64</a></sup>. Uniquely aligned read counts were calculated for each gene for each tissue sample. We performed the exact conditional test of two Poisson rates on read counts of duplicated genes to test the differential expression of duplicated genes, according to the method applied in soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Roulin, A. et al. The fate of duplicated genes in a polyploid plant genome. Plant J. 73, 143â153 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4601">65</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Gu, K., Ng, H. K., Tang, M. L. &amp; Schucany, W. R. Testing the ratio of two poisson rates. Biom. J. 50, 283â298 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4604">66</a></sup>. For each duplicated gene pair (for example, genes A and B), read counts and gene length were denoted as Ea and La for gene A, and Eb and Lb for gene B, respectively. The read counts of the genes A and B were assumed to follow the Poisson distributions with rates <i>Î»A</i>=Ra Ã La and <i>Î»B</i>=Rb Ã Lb. Under the null hypothesis of equal expression of the genes A and B, that is, Ra=Rb, the conditional distribution of Ea given Ea+Eb=<i>k</i> follows a binomial distribution with success probability <i>P</i>=Î»a/(Î»a+Î»b)=La/(La+Lb). The <i>P</i> values were computed and further adjusted to maintain the false discovery rate at 0.05 across gene pairs using the BenjaminiâHochberg method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Benjamini, Y. &amp; Hochberg, Y. Controlling the false discovery rate: a practical and powerful approach to multiple testing. J. Roy. Statist. Soc. Ser. 57, 289â300 (1995)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4624">67</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Statistical analysis</h3><p>The average number of all retained orthologues in the three subgenomes was used to estimate the expected retained gene number in each block, and used together with the observed retained gene number, for the gene retention disparity statistics using the <i>Ï</i><sup>2</sup> test. In the GO, IPR (Interproscan) or KEGG enrichment analyses of WGT or tandem genes, the <i>Ï</i><sup>2</sup> test (<i>N</i>&gt;5) or the Fisherâs exact test (<i>N</i>â¤5) was used to detect significant differences between the proportion of (WGT or tandem) genes observed in each child GO, IPR or KEGG categories, and the expected overall proportion of (WGT or tandem) genes in the whole genome. Correlation of the gene numbers of WGT-derived paralogous genes with tandem genes in 938 GO terms was tested by Pearson correlation coefficients (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4930#MOESM527">Supplementary Figure 68</a>). The BenjaminiâHochberg false discovery rate was performed to adjust the <i>P</i> values<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Benjamini, Y. &amp; Hochberg, Y. Controlling the false discovery rate: a practical and powerful approach to multiple testing. J. Roy. Statist. Soc. Ser. 57, 289â300 (1995)." href="/articles/ncomms4930#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d21598e4657">67</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec21" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec21-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec21">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec21-content"><p><b>Accession codes:</b> Genome sequence data for <i>B. oleracea</i> have been deposited in the DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank nucleotide core database under the accession code AOIX00000000. Transcriptome sequence data for <i>B. rapa and B. oleracea</i> have been deposited in the DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank Sequence Read Archive (SRA) under the accession codes GSE<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=</a>43245 and GSE<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=42891">42891</a> respectively.</p><p><b>How to cite this article:</b> Liu, S. <i>et al</i>. The <i>Brassica oleracea</i> genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes. <i>Nat. Commun.</i> 5:3930 doi: 10.1038/ncomms4930 (2014).</p></div></div></section>

              </h3>

                    class="c-article-further-reading__authors-list js-list-authors-3 js-etal-collapsed" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"
